				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lilly_(Eli)_%26_Co.&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Lilly (Eli) &amp; Co.">Lilly (Eli) &amp; Co.</a>)</span></div>

			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Eli_Lilly_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Eli Lilly (disambiguation)">Eli Lilly (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<p><b>Eli Lilly and Company</b> is an American <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> company headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis,_Indiana" class="mw-redirect" title="Indianapolis, Indiana">Indianapolis, Indiana</a>, with offices in 18 countries. Its products are sold in approximately 125 countries. The company was founded in 1876 by, and named after, Col. <a href="/wiki/Eli_Lilly" title="Eli Lilly">Eli Lilly</a>, a pharmaceutical <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> and veteran of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>.

class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Lilly's notable achievements include being the first company to mass-produce the <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">polio vaccine</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Salk" title="Jonas Salk">Jonas Salk</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>. It was one of the first <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> companies to produce human <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> using <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant DNA</a> including Humulin (<a href="/wiki/Insulin_(medication)" title="Insulin (medication)">insulin medication</a>), Humalog (<a href="/wiki/Insulin_lispro" title="Insulin lispro">insulin lispro</a>), and the first approved <a href="/wiki/Biosimilar" title="Biosimilar">biosimilar</a> insulin product in the US, Basaglar (<a href="/wiki/Insulin_glargine" title="Insulin glargine">insulin glargine</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p><p>Lilly is currently the largest manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatric</a> medications and produces Prozac (<a href="/wiki/Fluoxetine" title="Fluoxetine">fluoxetine</a>), Dolophine (<a href="/wiki/Methadone" title="Methadone">methadone</a>), Cymbalta (<a href="/wiki/Duloxetine" title="Duloxetine">duloxetine</a>), and Zyprexa (<a href="/wiki/Olanzapine" title="Olanzapine">olanzapine</a>). According to John Marks, the author of The Search For The Manchurian Candidate, Lilly also produced LSD for the CIA’s <a href="/wiki/Project_MKUltra" title="Project MKUltra">MKultra</a> mind control program.
</p><p>The company is ranked 123rd on the 2019 <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It is ranked 221st on the <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Global_2000" title="Forbes Global 2000">Forbes Global 2000</a> list of the largest public companies in the world<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and 252nd on the Forbes list of America's Best Employers.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p><p>Eli Lilly is a full member of the <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_Research_and_Manufacturers_of_America" title="Pharmaceutical Research and Manufacturers of America">Pharmaceutical Research and Manufacturers of America</a><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Federation_of_Pharmaceutical_Industries_and_Associations" title="European Federation of Pharmaceutical Industries and Associations">European Federation of Pharmaceutical Industries and Associations</a> (EFPIA).<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p><p>As of 1997, it was the largest corporation and the largest charitable benefactor in Indiana.<sup id="cite_ref-p58_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p58-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none"><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Company_founder">Company founder</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Company founder" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eli_Lilly" title="Eli Lilly">Eli Lilly</a></div>
<p>The company's founder was Colonel <a href="/wiki/Eli_Lilly" title="Eli Lilly">Eli Lilly</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Pharmacist" title="Pharmacist">pharmaceutical chemist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union army</a> veteran of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. Lilly served as the company president until his death in 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR1_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>In 1869, after working for drugstores in Indiana, Lilly became a partner in a <a href="/wiki/Paris,_Illinois" title="Paris, Illinois">Paris, Illinois</a>, drugstore with James W. Binford.<sup id="cite_ref-b911_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b911-11">[11]</a></sup> In 1873, Lilly left the partnership with Binford and returned to Indianapolis. In 1874, Lilly partnered with John F. Johnston and opened a drug manufacturing operation called Johnston and Lilly, but dissolved the partnership in 1876. Lilly used his share of the assets to open his own pharmaceutical manufacturing business in Indianapolis in May 1876. His new business venture became Eli Lilly and Company.<sup id="cite_ref-LillyBio_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LillyBio-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-m6_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m6-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonel_eli_lilly_in_1885.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Colonel_eli_lilly_in_1885.JPG/220px-Colonel_eli_lilly_in_1885.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="246" data-file-height="245"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 219px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Colonel_eli_lilly_in_1885.JPG/220px-Colonel_eli_lilly_in_1885.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="219" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonel_eli_lilly_in_1885.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colonel <a href="/wiki/Eli_Lilly" title="Eli Lilly">Eli Lilly</a> (1838–1898), founder</div></div></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Early_days:_1870.E2.80.931900"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_days:_1870–1900">Early days: 1870–1900</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early days: 1870–1900" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On May 10, 1876, Lilly opened his own laboratory in Indianapolis, where he began to manufacture medicinal drugs. The sign outside, above the shop's door, read: "Eli Lilly, Chemist."<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR1_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR1-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b540_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b540-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Lilly began his manufacturing venture with three employees including Lilly's son, <a href="/wiki/Josiah_K._Lilly_Sr." title="Josiah K. Lilly Sr.">Josiah</a> (J. K.).<sup id="cite_ref-p59_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p59-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-m6_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m6-13">[13]</a></sup></p><p>One of the first medicines that Lilly produced was <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">quinine</a>, a drug used to treat <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-p57_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p57-17">[17]</a></sup> By the end of 1876, sales reached $4,470.<sup id="cite_ref-p57_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p57-17">[17]</a></sup> By 1879, the company had grown to $48,000.<sup id="cite_ref-p57_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p57-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1878, Lilly hired his brother, James, as his first full-time salesman and the subsequent sales team marketed the company's drugs nationally.<sup id="cite_ref-Kahn23_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kahn23-18">[18]</a></sup></p><p>The company moved from Pearl Street to larger quarters at 36 South Meridian Street. In 1881, the company moved to its headquarters in Indianapolis's south-side industrial area. Lilly later purchased additional facilities for research and production.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR14_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR14-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-m27_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m27-20">[20]</a></sup></p><p>Lilly's first innovation was gelatin-coating for pills and capsules. The company's other early innovations included fruit flavorings and sugarcoated pills, which made the medicines easier to swallow.<sup id="cite_ref-p59_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p59-16">[16]</a></sup></p><p>In 1881, Lilly formally incorporated the business as Eli Lilly and Company, elected a board of directors, and issued stock to family members and close associates.<sup id="cite_ref-Kahn23_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kahn23-18">[18]</a></sup> Colonel Lilly's only son, Josiah (J. K.), a pharmaceutical chemist, graduated from the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_College_of_Pharmacy_and_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia College of Pharmacy and Science">Philadelphia College of Pharmacy</a> in 1882, and joined the family business as a superintendent of its laboratory after college. J. K. became company president in 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-b913_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b913-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1883 the company contracted to mix and sell Succus Alteran, its first widely successful product and one its best sellers. The product was marketed as a "blood purifier" and as a treatment for <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>, some types of rheumatism, and skin diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Eczema" class="mw-redirect" title="Eczema">eczema</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psoriasis" title="Psoriasis">psoriasis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-m27_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m27-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM72_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM72-22">[22]</a></sup> Sales from this product provided funds for Lilly to expand its manufacturing and research facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR2_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR2-23">[23]</a></sup> By the late 1880s Colonel Lilly was one of the Indianapolis area's leading businessmen, whose company had more than one-hundred employees and had $200,000 ($5,276,296 in 2015 chained dollars) in annual sales.<sup id="cite_ref-b911_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b911-11">[11]</a></sup></p><p>As the Lilly company grew, other businesses set up operations near the plant on Indianapolis's near south side. The area developed into one of the city's major business and industrial hubs. Lilly's production, manufacturing, research, and administrative operations in Indianapolis eventually occupied a complex of more than two dozen buildings covering a fifteen-block area, as well as production plants along Kentucky Avenue.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_Medicines,_1906.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Eli_Lilly_Medicines%2C_1906.jpg/220px-Eli_Lilly_Medicines%2C_1906.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="253" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="574"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 253px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Eli_Lilly_Medicines%2C_1906.jpg/220px-Eli_Lilly_Medicines%2C_1906.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="253" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_Medicines,_1906.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An assortment of Lilly's <a href="/wiki/Throat_lozenge" title="Throat lozenge">throat lozenges</a> from a 1906 sales book.</div></div></div>
<p>Around 1890, Colonel Lilly turned over the day-to-day management of the business to his son, J. K., who ran the company for thirty-four years. Although the 1890s were a tumultuous decade economically, the company flourished and came out stronger than ever.<sup id="cite_ref-b911_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b911-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-p60_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p60-25">[25]</a></sup> In 1894 Lilly purchased a manufacturing plant to be used solely for creating capsules. The company also made several technological advances in the manufacturing process, including automating its capsule production. Over the next few years the company annually created tens of millions of capsules and pills.<sup id="cite_ref-PodczekJones_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PodczekJones-26">[26]</a></sup></p><p>Until the turn of the century Lilly operated in Indianapolis and the surrounding area as many other pharmaceutical businesses did—manufacturing and selling "sugar-coated pills, fluid extracts, elixirs, and syrups".<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM72_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM72-22">[22]</a></sup> The company used plants for its raw materials and produced its products by hand. One historian noted, "Although the Indianapolis firm was more careful in making and promoting drugs than the patent medicine men of the era, the company remained ambivalent about scientific research."<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM72_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM72-22">[22]</a></sup></p><p>In addition to Colonel Lilly, his brother, James, and son, Josiah (J. K.), the growing company employed other Lilly family. Colonel Lilly's cousin, Evan Lilly, was hired as a bookkeeper.<sup id="cite_ref-p60_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p60-25">[25]</a></sup> As young boys, Lilly's grandsons, <a href="/wiki/Eli_Lilly_(industrialist)" title="Eli Lilly (industrialist)">Eli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josiah_K._Lilly,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Josiah K. Lilly, Jr.">Josiah Jr.</a> (Joe), ran errands and performed other odd jobs. Eli and Joe joined the family business after college. Eventually, each grandson served as company president and chairman of the board.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR256_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR256-27">[27]</a></sup></p><p>Josiah (J. K.), Colonel Lilly's son and Eli and Joe's father, inherited the company after Colonel Lilly died and became its president in 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR3_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR3-28">[28]</a></sup> At the time of Colonel Lilly's death the company had a product line of 2,005 items and annual sales of more than $300,000 ($8,547,600 in 2015 chained dollars).<sup id="cite_ref-bb912_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bb912-29">[29]</a></sup> Colonel Lilly was a pioneer in the modern pharmaceutical industry, with many of his early innovations later becoming standard practice. His ethical reforms in a trade that was marked by outlandish claims of miracle medicines began a period of rapid advancement in the development of medicinal drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> J. K. Lilly continued to advocate for federal regulation on medicines.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Under J. K.'s leadership, the company introduced scientific management concepts, organized the company's research department, increased its sales force, and began international distribution of its products.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSindustry3_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSindustry3-32">[32]</a></sup> In addition J. K. oversaw a large expansion of the company. By 1905 the company reached sales of $1 million ($26,381,481 in 2015 chained dollars).<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR3_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR3-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Josiah_K._Lilly.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/Josiah_K._Lilly.jpg" decoding="async" width="202" height="315" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="202" data-file-height="315"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 202px;height: 315px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/42/Josiah_K._Lilly.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="202" data-height="315" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Josiah_K._Lilly.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Josiah_K._Lilly,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Josiah K. Lilly, Sr.">Josiah K. Lilly, Sr.</a> (1861–1948), second company president.</div></div></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Modernization:1900.E2.80.931940"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernization:1900–1940">Modernization:1900–1940</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Modernization:1900–1940" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_and_Company_Headquarters_ca1919_b1007511_003_tif_zs25x9459.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Eli_Lilly_and_Company_Headquarters_ca1919_b1007511_003_tif_zs25x9459.png/220px-Eli_Lilly_and_Company_Headquarters_ca1919_b1007511_003_tif_zs25x9459.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4850" data-file-height="3112"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 141px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Eli_Lilly_and_Company_Headquarters_ca1919_b1007511_003_tif_zs25x9459.png/220px-Eli_Lilly_and_Company_Headquarters_ca1919_b1007511_003_tif_zs25x9459.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="141" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_and_Company_Headquarters_ca1919_b1007511_003_tif_zs25x9459.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eli Lilly and Company Headquarters, Indianapolis, ca.1919</div></div></div>
<p>Just before and after World War I, the Lilly company experienced rapid change.<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM72_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM72-22">[22]</a></sup> Expansion of Lilly's manufacturing facilities at the McCarty Street plant improved production capacity with a new Science Building (Building 14), opened in 1911, and a new capsule plant (Building 15) in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The company also began constructions of the <a href="/wiki/Lilly_Biological_Laboratories" title="Lilly Biological Laboratories">Lilly Biological Laboratories</a>, a research and manufacturing plant on 150 acres near Greenfield, Indiana, in 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SHAARD_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SHAARD-35">[35]</a></sup></p><p>In addition to development of new medicines, the company achieved several technological advances, including automation of its production facilities. Lilly was also an innovator in pill capsule manufacturing. It was among the first manufacturers to insert medications into empty gelatin capsules, which provided a more exact dosage.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR1_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR1-10">[10]</a></sup> Lilly manufactured capsules for its own needs and sold its excess capacity to others.<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM73_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM73-36">[36]</a></sup> A 1917 <i>Scientific American</i> article claimed the Lilly operation in Indianapolis was "the largest capsule factory in the world" and was "capable of producing 2.5 million capsules a day".<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM73_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM73-36">[36]</a></sup> One of Lilly's innovations was fruit flavoring for medicines and sugar-coated pills to make their medicines easier to swallow.<sup id="cite_ref-p59_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p59-16">[16]</a></sup> Over the next few years the company began to create tens of millions of capsules and pills annually.<sup id="cite_ref-PodczekJones_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PodczekJones-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Belladonna_cultivation_Hand_book_of_pharmacy_and_therapeutics_b1007511_005_tif_5x21tg304.tiff" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Belladonna_cultivation_Hand_book_of_pharmacy_and_therapeutics_b1007511_005_tif_5x21tg304.tiff/lossless-page1-220px-Belladonna_cultivation_Hand_book_of_pharmacy_and_therapeutics_b1007511_005_tif_5x21tg304.tiff.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="341" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3704" data-file-height="5737"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 341px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Belladonna_cultivation_Hand_book_of_pharmacy_and_therapeutics_b1007511_005_tif_5x21tg304.tiff/lossless-page1-220px-Belladonna_cultivation_Hand_book_of_pharmacy_and_therapeutics_b1007511_005_tif_5x21tg304.tiff.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="341" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Belladonna_cultivation_Hand_book_of_pharmacy_and_therapeutics_b1007511_005_tif_5x21tg304.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Belladonna cultivation, Eli Lilly and Company, 1919</div></div></div>
<p>Other advances improved plant efficiency and eliminated production errors. Eli Lilly, grandson of the company founder, introduced a method for blueprinting manufacturing tickets in 1909.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This process, which created multiples copies of a drug formula, helped eliminate manufacturing and transcription errors.<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM73_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM73-36">[36]</a></sup> In the 1920s Eli introduced the new concept of straight-line production, where raw materials entered at one end of the facility and the finished product came out the other end, in the company's manufacturing process. Under Eli's supervision, the design for Building 22, a new 5-floor plant that opened in Indianapolis in 1926, implemented the straight-line concept to improve production efficiency and lower production costs.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM74_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM74-39">[39]</a></sup> One historian noted, "It was probably the most sophisticated production system in the American pharmaceutical industry."<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM74_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM74-39">[39]</a></sup> This more efficient manufacturing process also allowed the company to hire a regular workforce. Instead of recalling workers at peak times and laying them off when production demand fell, Lilly's regular workforce produced less-costly medicines in off-peak times using the same manufacturing facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR256_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR256-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Views_in_Elastic_Filled_Capsule_Department_b1007511_008_tif_bn999778q.tiff" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Views_in_Elastic_Filled_Capsule_Department_b1007511_008_tif_bn999778q.tiff/lossless-page1-220px-Views_in_Elastic_Filled_Capsule_Department_b1007511_008_tif_bn999778q.tiff.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="362" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2946" data-file-height="4849"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 362px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Views_in_Elastic_Filled_Capsule_Department_b1007511_008_tif_bn999778q.tiff/lossless-page1-220px-Views_in_Elastic_Filled_Capsule_Department_b1007511_008_tif_bn999778q.tiff.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="362" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Views_in_Elastic_Filled_Capsule_Department_b1007511_008_tif_bn999778q.tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Men and women workers preparing drug capsules, Eli Lilly and Company, 1919</div></div></div>
<p>During the 1920s the introduction of new products also brought the company financial success.<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM72_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM72-22">[22]</a></sup> In 1919 Josiah hired biochemist <a href="/wiki/George_Henry_Alexander_Clowes" title="George Henry Alexander Clowes">George Henry Alexander Clowes</a> as director of biochemical research. In 1921 three <a href="/wiki/University_of_Toronto" title="University of Toronto">University of Toronto</a> scientists, <a href="/wiki/J._J._R._Macleod" class="mw-redirect" title="J. J. R. Macleod">J. J. R. Macleod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_G._Banting" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick G. Banting">Frederick G. Banting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_H._Best" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles H. Best">Charles H. Best</a>, were working on the development of <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> for treatment of diabetes.<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM76_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM76-40">[40]</a></sup> Clowes proposed a collaboration with the researchers in December 1921, then again March and May 1922. The researchers were hesitant to work with a commercial drug firm, particularly since they had the <a href="/wiki/Connaught_Laboratories" title="Connaught Laboratories">Connaught Laboratories</a>' non-commercial facilities at hand. Nonetheless, as limits were reached at the scale to which Connaught could produce insulin, Clowes and Eli Lilly met with the researchers in 1922 to negotiate an agreement with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Toronto" title="University of Toronto">University of Toronto</a> scientists to mass-produce insulin.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The collaboration greatly accelerated the large-scale production of the extract.<sup id="cite_ref-Roberts_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-44">[44]</a></sup> In 1923, Lilly began selling Iletin (Insulin, Lilly), their tradename for the first commercially available insulin product in the U.S for the treatment of diabetes.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly61_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly61-45">[45]</a></sup> Numerous objections were registered by the Insulin Committee of the University of Toronto in regard to Lilly's use of the term "Iletin", although production continued under this name and the objection was later dropped "as a concession".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p><p>Banting and Macleod won a Nobel Prize in 1923 for their research, which they subsequently shared with co-discoverers <a href="/wiki/Charles_Best_(medical_scientist)" title="Charles Best (medical scientist)">Charles Best</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Collip" title="James Collip">James Collip</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Insulin, "the most important drug" in the company's history, did "more than any other" to make Lilly "one of the major pharmaceutical manufacturers in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM76_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM76-40">[40]</a></sup> Eli Lilly enjoyed effective monopoly on the sale of insulin in the U.S. for almost two years, until the first of the new American licensees, Frederick Stearns &amp; Co., entered the market in June 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p><p>The success of insulin enabled the company to attract well-respected scientists and, with them, make more medical advances. By its fiftieth anniversary in 1926 sales reached $9 million and the company produced more than 2,800 different items.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR256_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR256-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1928 Lilly introduced Liver Extract 343 for the treatment of pernicious anemia, a blood disorder, in a joint venture with two <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> scientists, <a href="/wiki/George_R._Minot" class="mw-redirect" title="George R. Minot">George R. Minot</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_P._Murphy" title="William P. Murphy">William P. Murphy</a>. In 1930 Lilly introduced Liver Extract No. 55 in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/George_Whipple" title="George Whipple">George Whipple</a>, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rochester" title="University of Rochester">University of Rochester</a> scientist.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Minot, Murphy, and Whipple won the 1934 Nobel Prize in medicine for their research.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly67_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly67-52">[52]</a></sup></p><p>Despite the economic challenges of the Great Depression, Lilly's sales rose to $13 million in 1932.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR5_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR5-53">[53]</a></sup> That same year <a href="/wiki/Eli_Lilly_(industrialist)" title="Eli Lilly (industrialist)">Eli Lilly</a>, the eldest grandson of Col. Lilly, was named as the company's president to succeed his father, who remained as chairman of the board until 1948. Eli joined the family business in 1909.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR3_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR3-28">[28]</a></sup> In his early years at the company Eli was especially interested in improving production efficiency and introduced a number of labor-saving devices. He also introduced scientific management principles and implemented cost-savings measures that modernized the company.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In addition Eli was involved in expanding the company's research efforts and collaborations with university researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p><p>In 1934, the firm opened two new facilities on the McCarty Street complex: a replica of Lilly's 1876 laboratory and the new <a href="/wiki/Lilly_Research_Laboratories" class="mw-redirect" title="Lilly Research Laboratories">Lilly Research Laboratories</a>, "one of the most fully equipped facilities in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In the 1930s the company also continued expansion overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly111_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly111-57">[57]</a></sup> In 1934, Eli Lilly and Company Limited, the company's first overseas subsidiary was established in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, with headquarters in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> and a manufacturing plant in <a href="/wiki/Basingstoke,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Basingstoke, England">Basingstoke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR5_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR5-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MLilly111_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly111-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Expansion:_1940.E2.80.931970"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion:_1940–1970">Expansion: 1940–1970</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Expansion: 1940–1970" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> brought production at Lilly to a new high with the manufacturing of <a href="/wiki/Thiomersal" title="Thiomersal">Merthiolate</a> and penicillin. During the war Lilly also cooperated with the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> to process blood plasma and by war's end the company had dried over two million pints of blood, "about 20 percent of the United States' total".<sup id="cite_ref-Madison,_Eli_Lilly,_p._105_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madison,_Eli_Lilly,_p._105-58">[58]</a></sup> Merthiolate, first introduced in 1930, was an "anticeptic and germicide" that became a U.S. army "standard issue" during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MLilly65_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly65-60">[60]</a></sup> In the early 1940s Lilly became one of the companies mass-producing <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p><p>International operations expanded even further during World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly111_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly111-57">[57]</a></sup> Eli Lilly International Corp. was formed in 1943 as a subsidiary to encourage business trade abroad. By 1948 Lilly employees worked in thirty-five countries, most of them as sales representatives in Latin America, Asia, and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly111_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly111-57">[57]</a></sup></p><p>At the end of World War II the company continued to grow. In 1945 Lilly began a major expansion effort that would include two manufacturing operations in Indianapolis. The company purchased the massive Curtiss-Wright propeller plant on South Kentucky Avenue, west of the company's McCarty Street operation. When renovation was completed in mid-1947, the Kentucky Avenue location manufactured antibiotics and capsules and housed the company's shipping department.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> By 1948 Lilly employed nearly 7,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-b540_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b540-14">[14]</a></sup></p><p>Eli Lilly, who had served as the company's president since 1932, retired from active management of the company in 1948, became chairman of the board, and relinquished the presidency to his brother, <a href="/wiki/Josiah_K._Lilly,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Josiah K. Lilly, Jr.">Josiah K. Lilly, Jr.</a> (Joe).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> During Eli's sixteen-year presidency sales rose from $13 million in 1932 to $117 million in 1948. Joe joined the company in 1914 and concentrated on the company's personnel and marketing efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-bb912_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bb912-29">[29]</a></sup> He served as company president from 1948 to 1953, then became chairman of the board and remained in that capacity until his death in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1952 the company offered its first public shares of stock.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In 1953 Eugene N. Beesley was named the company's new president, the first non-family member to run the company.
</p><p>Over the next several decades Lilly continued to develop and introduce new drugs to the marketplace. In the 1950s Lilly introduced two new antibiotics: vancomycin and erythromycin. In addition Lilly was heavily involved in production and distribution of <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Salk" title="Jonas Salk">Jonas Salk</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Polio_vaccine" title="Polio vaccine">poliomyelitis (polio) vaccine</a>. In 1954 the National Foundation for Infantile Paralysis (NFIP) contracted with five pharmaceutical companies to produce Salk's polio vaccine for clinical trials. These included Lilly as well as Parke, Davis and Company, Cutter Laboratories, Wyeth Laboratories, and Pitman-Moore Company.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSpolio5_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSpolio5-66">[66]</a></sup> Lilly's selection to produce the vaccine was, in part, due to its previous experience in collaborations with university researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSpolio6_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSpolio6-67">[67]</a></sup> Lilly manufactured 60 percent of the Salk vaccine in 1955.
</p><p>During the mid-twentieth century Lilly continued to expand its production facilities outside of Indianapolis. In 1950 Lilly began Tippecanoe Laboratories in <a href="/wiki/Lafayette,_Indiana" title="Lafayette, Indiana">Lafayette, Indiana</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-b541_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b541-68">[68]</a></sup> and increased <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotic</a> production with its patent on <a href="/wiki/Erythromycin" title="Erythromycin">erythromycin</a>. In 1954 Lilly formed <a href="/w/index.php?title=Elanco_Animal_Health&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Elanco Animal Health (page does not exist)">Elanco Products Company</a> for the production of <a href="/wiki/Veterinary" class="mw-redirect" title="Veterinary">veterinary</a> pharmaceuticals. In 1969, the company opened a new plant in <a href="/wiki/Clinton,_Indiana" title="Clinton, Indiana">Clinton, Indiana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-b541_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b541-68">[68]</a></sup></p><p>After a company reorganization and transition to non-family management in 1953, Lilly continued to expand its global presence. In the 1960s Lilly operated thirteen affiliate companies outside the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR6_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR6-69">[69]</a></sup> In 1962, with an acquisition from Distillers Company, the company established a major factory in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, England. In 1968, Lilly built its first research facility, the <a href="/wiki/Lilly_Research_Centre" title="Lilly Research Centre">Lilly Research Centre</a> Limited, outside the United States near <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London, England</a> in <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="1970.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1970–present">1970–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1970–present" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_Corporate_Center,_Indianapolis,_Indiana,_USA.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Eli_Lilly_Corporate_Center%2C_Indianapolis%2C_Indiana%2C_USA.jpg/220px-Eli_Lilly_Corporate_Center%2C_Indianapolis%2C_Indiana%2C_USA.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Eli_Lilly_Corporate_Center%2C_Indianapolis%2C_Indiana%2C_USA.jpg/220px-Eli_Lilly_Corporate_Center%2C_Indianapolis%2C_Indiana%2C_USA.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_Corporate_Center,_Indianapolis,_Indiana,_USA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eli Lilly and Company's Corporate Center in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> in 2019.</div></div></div>
<p>During the 1970s and 1980s, Eli Lilly and Company saw a flurry of drug production: an antibiotic, <a href="/wiki/Cefalexin" title="Cefalexin">Keflex</a>, in 1971; a heart drug, <a href="/wiki/Dobutamine" title="Dobutamine">Dobutrex</a>, in 1977; Ceclor, which would become the world's top selling oral antibiotic, in 1979; a <a href="/wiki/Leukemia" title="Leukemia">leukemia</a> drug, Eldisine; an antiarthritic, <a href="/wiki/Benoxaprofen" title="Benoxaprofen">Oraflex</a>; and an analgesic, <a href="/wiki/Dextropropoxyphene" title="Dextropropoxyphene">Darvon</a>. When generic drugs flooded the marketplace after the expiration of patents for drugs discovered in the 1950s and 1960s, Lilly diversified into other areas, most notably agricultural chemicals, animal-health products, cosmetics, and medical instruments.
</p><p>In 1971, the company became a component of the <a href="/wiki/S%26P_500_Index" title="S&amp;P 500 Index">S&amp;P 500 Index</a>.
</p><p>To further diversify its product line, Lilly made an uncharacteristic, but ultimately profitable move in 1971, when it bought cosmetic manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Arden,_Inc." title="Elizabeth Arden, Inc.">Elizabeth Arden, Inc.</a> for $38 million. Although the subsidiary continued to lose money for five years after Lilly acquired it, executive management changes at Arden helped turn it into a financial success. By 1982 the subsidiary's "sales were up 90 percent from 1978, with profits doubling to nearly $30 million." Sixteen years after its acquisition, Lilly sold Arden to <a href="/wiki/Faberg%C3%A9_(cosmetics)" title="Fabergé (cosmetics)">Fabergé</a> in 1987 for $657 million.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR7_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR7-70">[70]</a></sup></p><p>In 1977, Lilly ventured into medical instruments with the acquisition of IVAC Corporation, which manufactures vital signs and <a href="/wiki/Intravenous" class="mw-redirect" title="Intravenous">intravenous</a> fluid infusion monitoring systems.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Lilly also purchased Cardiac Pacemakers Incorporated, a manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_pacemaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial pacemaker">heart pacemakers</a> in 1977. In 1980, Lilly acquired Physio-Control Corporation. Other acquisitions included Advance Cardiovasular Systems Incorporated in 1984, Hybritech in 1986, and Devices for Vascular Intervention, Incorporated in 1989. Lilly acquired Pacific Biotech in 1990 and Origin Medsystems and Heart Rhythm Technologies, Incorporated in 1992. In the early 1990s, Lilly combined these medical equipment companies into a Medical Devices and Diagnostics Division that "contributed about 20 percent" of Lilly's annual revenues.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>In 1989, a joint agri-chemical venture between Elanco Products Company and <a href="/wiki/Dow_Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Dow Chemical">Dow Chemical</a> created DowElanco. In 1997, Lilly sold its 40% share in the company to Dow Chemical for $1.2 billion and the name was changed to <a href="/wiki/Dow_AgroSciences" title="Dow AgroSciences">Dow AgroSciences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p><p>In 1994, Lilly acquired PCS Systems, a drug delivery business for Health Maintenance Organizations, and later added two similar organizations to its holdings.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR8_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR8-73">[73]</a></sup> Lilly purchased PCS, which was the largest U.S. prescription drug benefits manager at the time, for $4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Kokomo73194_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kokomo73194-74">[74]</a></sup></p><p>In 1991, Vaughn Bryson was named <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">CEO</a> of Eli Lilly. During his 20-month tenure, the company reported its first quarterly loss as a publicly traded company.<sup id="cite_ref-KokomoTrib62893_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KokomoTrib62893-75">[75]</a></sup> In 1993 <a href="/wiki/Randall_L._Tobias" title="Randall L. Tobias">Randall L. Tobias</a>, a vice-chairman of <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Corporation" title="AT&amp;T Corporation">AT&amp;T Corporation</a> and Lilly board member, was named Lilly's chairman, president, and CEO after "product and competitive pressures" had "steadily eroded Lilly's stock price since early 1992."<sup id="cite_ref-KokomoTrib62693_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KokomoTrib62693-76">[76]</a></sup> Tobias was the first president and CEO recruited from outside of the company. Under Tobias's leadership the company "cut costs and narrowed its mission".<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR89_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR89-77">[77]</a></sup> Lilly sold companies in its Medical Device and Diagnostics Division, expanded international sales, made new acquisitions, and funded additional research and product development. <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Taurel" title="Sidney Taurel">Sidney Taurel</a>, former chief operating officer of Lilly, was named CEO in 1998, replacing Tobias. Taurel was named chairman in January 1999. In 2000 Lilly reported $10.86 billion in net sales.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_global_manufacturing_plants.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Eli_Lilly_global_manufacturing_plants.png/220px-Eli_Lilly_global_manufacturing_plants.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 96px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Eli_Lilly_global_manufacturing_plants.png/220px-Eli_Lilly_global_manufacturing_plants.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="96" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eli_Lilly_global_manufacturing_plants.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eli Lilly's present day global manufacturing plants.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1998, Eli Lilly formed a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Icos" title="Icos">Icos Corporation</a> (ICOS), a <a href="/wiki/Bothell,_Washington" title="Bothell, Washington">Bothell, Washington</a>-based biotechnology company, to develop and commercialize <a href="/wiki/Tadalafil" title="Tadalafil">Cialis</a>, a product for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Erectile_dysfunction" title="Erectile dysfunction">erectile dysfunction</a>. In October 2006, Lilly announced its intention to acquire Icos for $2.1 billion, or $32 per share.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> After its initial attempt to acquire Icos failed under pressure from large institutional shareholders, Lilly revised its offer to $34 per share. Institutional Shareholder Services (ISS), a proxy advisory firm, advised Icos shareholders to reject the proposal as undervalued,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TimmermanProposed_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimmermanProposed-80">[80]</a></sup> but the buyout was approved by Icos shareholders and Lilly completed its acquisition of the company on January 29, 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Lilly closed Icos manufacturing operations, terminated nearly 500 Icos employees, and left 127 employees working at the biologics facility.<sup id="cite_ref-Tartakoff_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tartakoff-83">[83]</a></sup> In December 2007, CMC Biopharmaceuticals A/S (CMC), a <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>-based provider of contract biomanufacturing services, bought the Bothell-based biologics facility from Lilly and retained the existing 127 employees.<sup id="cite_ref-TimmermanProposed_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TimmermanProposed-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tartakoff_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tartakoff-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2009, the largest criminal <a href="/wiki/Fine_(penalty)" title="Fine (penalty)">fine</a> in U.S. history, totaling $1.415 billion was imposed on Lilly for illegal marketing of its best-selling product, the <a href="/wiki/Atypical_antipsychotic" title="Atypical antipsychotic">atypical antipsychotic</a> medication, <a href="/wiki/Olanzapine" title="Olanzapine">Zyprexa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2011, <a href="/wiki/Boehringer_Ingelheim" title="Boehringer Ingelheim">Boehringer Ingelheim</a> and Eli Lilly and Company announced their global agreement for the joint development and marketing of new APIs for diabetes therapy. Lilly could receive more than one billion dollars for their work on the project, while Boehringer Ingelheim could receive more than $800 million from development of the new drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Oral anti-diabetic of Boehringer Ingelheim–<a href="/wiki/Linagliptin" title="Linagliptin">Linagliptin</a> and BI 10773–and two <a href="/wiki/Insulin_analog" title="Insulin analog">insulin analogs</a> of Lilly–LY2605541 and LY2963016–were in phase II and III of clinical developmentat that time.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2014, Lilly announced plans to buy <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Swiss</a> drugmaker <a href="/wiki/Novartis_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Novartis AG">Novartis AG</a>'s animal health business for $5.4 billion in cash to strengthen and diversify its Elanco unit. Lilly said it planned to fund the deal with about $3.4 billion of cash on hand and $2 billion of loans.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> As a condition of the acquisition, the <a href="/wiki/Milbemycin_oxime/lufenuron" title="Milbemycin oxime/lufenuron">Sentinel heartworm treatment</a> would be divested to <a href="/wiki/Virbac" title="Virbac">Virbac</a> in order to avoid a monopoly in a subsector of the heartworm (<a href="/wiki/Dirofilaria_immitis" title="Dirofilaria immitis">Dirofilaria immitis</a>) treatment market.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2015, the company announced it would join <a href="/wiki/Hanmi_Pharmaceutical" title="Hanmi Pharmaceutical">Hanmi Pharmaceutical</a> in developing and commercialising Hanmi's phase I <a href="/wiki/Bruton%27s_tyrosine_kinase" title="Bruton's tyrosine kinase">Bruton's tyrosine kinase</a> inhibitor <a href="/wiki/HM71224" class="mw-redirect" title="HM71224">HM71224</a> in a deal which could yield $690 million.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> A day later the company announced another deal with Innovent Biologics to co-develop and commercialise at least three of Innovents treatments over the next decade, in a deal which could generate up to $456 million. As part of the deal the company will contribute its c-Met monoclonal antibody whilst Innovent will contribute a monoclonal antibody which targets <a href="/w/index.php?title=CD-20&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="CD-20 (page does not exist)">CD-20</a>. The second compound from Innovent is a preclinical immuno-oncology molecule.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The following week the company announced it would restart its collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a> surrounding the Phase III trial of <a href="/wiki/Tanezumab" title="Tanezumab">Tanezumab</a>. Pfizer is expected to receive an upfront sum of $200 million from the company.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In April 2015, the company engaged <a href="/wiki/CBRE_Group" title="CBRE Group">CBRE Group</a> to sell its <a href="/wiki/Biomanufacturing" title="Biomanufacturing">biomanufacturing</a> facility in <a href="/wiki/Vacaville,_California" title="Vacaville, California">Vacaville, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plantsale2015_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantsale2015-93">[93]</a></sup>  The facility resides on a 52 acres (0.21 km<sup>2</sup>) campus and is one of the largest biopharmaceutical manufacturing centers in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-plantsale2015_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantsale2015-93">[93]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2017, Elanco Animal Health, a subsidiary of the company completed the acquisition of Boehringer Ingelheim Vetmedica, Inc's (a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Boehringer_Ingelheim" title="Boehringer Ingelheim">Boehringer Ingelheim</a>) US feline, canine and rabies vaccines portfolio.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2017, the company acquired CoLucid Pharmaceuticals for $960 million, specifically gaining the late clinical-stage migraine therapy candidate, <a href="/wiki/Lasmiditan" title="Lasmiditan">lasmiditan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2018, the company acquired <a href="/wiki/Armo_Biosciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Armo Biosciences">Armo Biosciences</a> for $1.6 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Days later the company announced it would acquire <a href="/wiki/Aurora_kinase_A" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurora kinase A">Aurora kinase A</a> inhibitor developer - AurKa Pharma - and control over the lead compound, <a href="/w/index.php?title=AK-01&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="AK-01 (page does not exist)">AK-01</a>, for up to $575 million.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2019, Eli Lilly announced it would acquire Loxo Oncology for $235 per share - valuing the business at around $8 billion - significantly expanding the business' oncology offerings. The deal will give Eli Lilly Loxo's oral <a href="/wiki/Trk_receptor" title="Trk receptor">TRK</a> inhibitor, <a href="/wiki/Vitrakvi" class="mw-redirect" title="Vitrakvi">Vitrakvi</a> (larotrectinib), LOXO-292, an oral proto-<a href="/wiki/Oncogene" title="Oncogene">oncogene</a> <a href="/wiki/Receptor_tyrosine_kinase" title="Receptor tyrosine kinase">receptor tyrosine kinase</a> rearranged during transfection (RET) inhibitor, LOXO-305, an oral <a href="/wiki/Bruton%27s_tyrosine_kinase" title="Bruton's tyrosine kinase">Bruton's tyrosine kinase</a> (BTK) inhibitor and LOXO-195, a follow-on TRK inhibitor.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In August 2019, Elanco acquired the Bayer animal health business for $7.6 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2020, the company announced its acquisition of Dermira for $1.1 billion, gaining control of two key assets, among others; <a href="/wiki/Lebrikizumab" title="Lebrikizumab">lebrikizumab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glycopyrronium" class="mw-redirect" title="Glycopyrronium">glycopyrronium</a> cloth used to treat <a href="/wiki/Hyperhidrosis" title="Hyperhidrosis">hyperhidrosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_history">Acquisition history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Acquisition history" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_history_2">Acquisition history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Acquisition history" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:1px #aaa solid;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:center;;">Eli Lilly and Company Acquisitions</div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">
