</div></div>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaborative_research">Collaborative research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Collaborative research" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<p>Eli Lilly and Company has a long history of collaboration with research scientists. In 1886 Ernest G. Eberhardt, a chemist, joined the company as its first full-time research scientist.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR12_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR12-117">[117]</a></sup> Lilly also hired two botanists, Walter H. Evans and John S. Wright, to join its early research efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR2_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR2-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> After World War I the company's expanded production facilities and introduction of new management methods set the stage for Lilly's next crucial phase—its "aggressive entry into scientific research and development."<sup id="cite_ref-b540_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b540-14">[14]</a></sup> The first big step came in 1919 when Josiah Lilly hired biochemist George Henry Alexander Clowes as director of biochemical research.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Clowes had extensive medical research expertise and links to the scientific research community, which led to the company's collaborations with researchers in the U.S. and elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly55_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly55-120">[120]</a></sup> Clowes's first major collaboration with researchers who developed <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Toronto" title="University of Toronto">University of Toronto</a> significantly impacted the company's future.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly55_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly55-120">[120]</a></sup> Lilly's success with insulin production secured the company's position as a leading research-based pharmaceutical manufacturer, allowing it to attract and hire more research scientists and to collaborate with other universities in additional medical research.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In 1934 the company built a new research laboratory in Indianapolis.<sup id="cite_ref-MadisonM76_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MadisonM76-40">[40]</a></sup> As part of its research and product development process Lilly also conducted clinical studies at Indianapolis City Hospital (Wishard Memorial Hospital). Lilly continues to conduct clinical studies to test medications before their introduction to the market.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR4_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR4-122">[122]</a></sup> In 1949 Eli Lilly actually went into partnership with the United States Army Reserve setting up a local Strategic Intelligence Research and Analysis (SIRA)Unit to allow employees to research company data for the Scientific Logistics and Eurasian fields of study (source: declassified Defense Intelligence Agency document MDR -0191-2008 dated 17 Sep 2012). In 1998 the company dedicated new laboratories for clinical research at the Indiana University Medical Center in Indianapolis.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Publicly_funded_research">Publicly funded research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Publicly funded research" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In addition to internal research and development activities, Lilly is also involved in publicly funded research projects with other industrial and academic partners. One example in the area of <a href="/wiki/Pre-clinical_development" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-clinical development">non-clinical</a> safety assessment is the InnoMed PredTox, a collaboration with pharmaceutical companies, research organizations, and the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> to improve the safety of drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Consortium_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consortium-124">[124]</a></sup> In 2008 this consortium, which included Lilly S.A. (Switzerland), secured a €8 million budget for a 40-month project that was coordinated by the European Federation of Pharmaceutical Industries and Associations (<a href="/wiki/EFPIA" class="mw-redirect" title="EFPIA">EFPIA</a>), an organization who represents the research-based pharmaceutical industry and biotech companies operating in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Consortium_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consortium-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-InnoMed_PredTox_official_project_website_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-InnoMed_PredTox_official_project_website-126">[126]</a></sup> In 2008 Lilly's activities included research projects within the framework of the <a href="/wiki/Innovative_Medicines_Initiative" title="Innovative Medicines Initiative">Innovative Medicines Initiative</a>, a public-private research initiative in Europe that is a joint effort of the <a href="/wiki/EFPIA" class="mw-redirect" title="EFPIA">EFPIA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IMI_Call_Topics_2008_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMI_Call_Topics_2008-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmaceutical_brands">Pharmaceutical brands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pharmaceutical brands" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<p>The company's most important products introduced prior to World War II included <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, which Lilly marketed as Iletin (Insulin, Lilly), Amytal, Merthiolate, ephedrine, and liver extracts.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly65_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly65-60">[60]</a></sup> Introduced in 1923, Iletin (Insulin, Lilly) was Lilly's first commercial insulin product. In 2002 the company was the leading producer of products for those with diabetes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>During World War II Lilly produced penicillins and other antibiotics. In addition to penicillin, other wartime production included "antimalarials," blood plasma, encephalitis vaccine, typhus and influenza vaccine, gas gangrene antitoxin, Merthiolate, and Iletin (Insulin, Lilly).<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p><p>Among the company's more recent pharmaceutical developments are <a href="/wiki/Cephalosporin" title="Cephalosporin">cephalosporin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erythromycin" title="Erythromycin">erythromycin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prozac" class="mw-redirect" title="Prozac">Prozac</a> (fluoxetine), a <a href="/wiki/Selective_serotonin_reuptake_inhibitor" title="Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor">selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor</a> (SSRI) for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical depression">clinical depression</a>. Ceclor, introduced in the 1970s, was an oral cephalosporin antibiotic. Prozac, introduced in the 1980s, quickly became the company's best-selling product for treatment of depression, but Lilly lost its U.S. patent protection for this product in 2001. Among other distinctions, Lilly is the world's largest manufacturer and distributor of medications used in a broad range of <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a>-related conditions, including <a href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Clinical depression">clinical depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Generalized_anxiety_disorder" title="Generalized anxiety disorder">generalized anxiety disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotic</a> addiction, <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>, and others.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical">Historical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Historical" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Eli Lilly has focused on patent-protected medicines, with generic manufacturers taking over production of earlier drugs whose patents have expired.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Cialis">Cialis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cialis" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tadalafil" title="Tadalafil">Tadalafil</a></div>
<p>In 2003, Eli Lilly introduced <a href="/wiki/Tadalafil" title="Tadalafil">Cialis</a> (tadalafil), a competitor to <a href="/wiki/Pfizer" title="Pfizer">Pfizer</a>'s blockbuster <a href="/wiki/Viagra" class="mw-redirect" title="Viagra">Viagra</a> for <a href="/wiki/Erectile_dysfunction" title="Erectile dysfunction">erectile dysfunction</a>. Cialis maintains an active period of 36 hours, causing it sometimes to be dubbed the "weekend pill". Cialis was developed in a partnership with <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> company Icos Corporation. On December 18, 2006, Lilly bought Icos in order to gain full control of the product.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Cymbalta">Cymbalta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cymbalta" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Duloxetine" title="Duloxetine">Duloxetine</a></div>
<p>Another Lilly manufactured anti-depressant, <a href="/wiki/Duloxetine" title="Duloxetine">Cymbalta</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Serotonin-norepinephrine_reuptake_inhibitor" class="mw-redirect" title="Serotonin-norepinephrine reuptake inhibitor">serotonin-norepinephrine reuptake inhibitor</a> used predominantly in the treatment of major depressive disorders and generalized anxiety disorder, ranks with Prozac as one of the most financially successful pharmaceuticals in industry history.  It is also used in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Fibromyalgia" title="Fibromyalgia">fibromyalgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuropathy" class="mw-redirect" title="Neuropathy">neuropathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronic_pain" title="Chronic pain">chronic pain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Gemzar">Gemzar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gemzar" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gemcitabine" title="Gemcitabine">Gemcitabine</a></div>
<p>In 1996, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Food and Drug Administration (United States)">U.S. Food and Drug Administration</a> approved <a href="/wiki/Gemcitabine" title="Gemcitabine">Gemzar</a> for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_cancer" title="Pancreatic cancer">pancreatic cancer</a>. Gemzar is commonly used in the treatment of pancreatic cancer, usually in coordination with 5-FU <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> and radiology. Gemzar also is routinely used in the treatment of non-small cell <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Methadone">Methadone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Methadone" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Methadone" title="Methadone">Methadone</a></div>
<p>Eli Lilly was the first distributor of <a href="/wiki/Methadone" title="Methadone">methadone</a> in the United States, an <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">analgesic</a> used frequently in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Narcotic" title="Narcotic">narcotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Drug_addiction" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug addiction">drug addictions</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Eli Lily was able to acquire the right to produce the drug commercially for just $1 because the patent rights of the original patent holders, <a href="/wiki/IG_Farben" title="IG Farben">IG Farben</a> and <a href="/wiki/Farbwerke_Hoechst" class="mw-redirect" title="Farbwerke Hoechst">Farbwerke Hoechst</a>, were not protected after the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies of World War II</a> seized all German patents, research records and trade names. Eli Lily introduced the drug to the United States in 1947, marketed under the trade name "Dolophine".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Prozac">Prozac</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Prozac" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fluoxetine" title="Fluoxetine">Fluoxetine</a></div>
<p>Prozac was one of the first therapies in its class to treat clinical depression by blocking the uptake of <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a>. Prozac was approved by the U.S. FDA in 1987 for use in treating depression, with generic versions appearing after 2002.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Secobarbital">Secobarbital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Secobarbital" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Secobarbital" title="Secobarbital">Secobarbital</a></div>
<p>Eli Lilly has manufactured <a href="/wiki/Secobarbital" title="Secobarbital">Secobarbital</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Barbiturate" title="Barbiturate">barbiturate</a> derivative with <a href="/wiki/Anaesthetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaesthetic">anaesthetic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anticonvulsant" title="Anticonvulsant">anticonvulsant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sedative" title="Sedative">sedative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hypnosis" title="Hypnosis">hypnotic</a> properties. Lilly marketed Secobarbital under the brand name Seconal. Secobarbital is indicated for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>, temporary <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a> and as a pre-operative medication to produce anaesthesia and <a href="/wiki/Anxiolysis" class="mw-redirect" title="Anxiolysis">anxiolysis</a> in short surgical, diagnostic, or therapeutic procedures which are minimally painful. With the onset of new therapies for the treatment of these conditions, Secobarbital has been less utilized, and Lilly ceased manufacturing it in 1999.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h5 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Secobarbital_overdoses">Secobarbital overdoses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Secobarbital overdoses" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h5>
<p>Secobarbital gained considerable attention during the 1970s, when it gained wide popularity as a <a href="/wiki/Recreational_drug_use" title="Recreational drug use">recreational drug</a>. On September 18, 1970, <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a> guitarist legend <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Jimi Hendrix</a> died from a secobarbital overdose. On June 22, 1969, secobarbital overdose was the cause of death of actress <a href="/wiki/Judy_Garland" title="Judy Garland">Judy Garland</a>. The drug was a central part of the plot of the hugely popular novel <i><a href="/wiki/Valley_of_the_Dolls" title="Valley of the Dolls">Valley of the Dolls</a></i> (1966) by <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Susann" title="Jacqueline Susann">Jacqueline Susann</a> in which three highly successful <a href="/wiki/Hollywood,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Hollywood, California">Hollywood</a> women each fall victim, in various ways, to the drug. The novel was later released as a film by the same name.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Thimerosal">Thimerosal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Thimerosal" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Thiomersal" title="Thiomersal">Thiomersal</a></div>
<p>Eli Lilly has developed the <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccine</a> preservative <a href="/wiki/Thiomersal" title="Thiomersal">thiomersal</a> (also called merthiolate and thimerosal). Thiomersal is effective by causing susceptible bacteria to <a href="/wiki/Autolysis_(biology)" title="Autolysis (biology)">autolyze</a>. Launched in 1930, merthiolate was a mercury-based antiseptic and germicide that "had been formulated at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Maryland">University of Maryland</a> with support of a Lilly research fellowship."<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly65_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly65-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Zyprexa">Zyprexa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Zyprexa" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Zyprexa (<a href="/wiki/Olanzapine" title="Olanzapine">Olanzapine</a>) (for <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Off-label" class="mw-redirect" title="Off-label">off-label</a> uses) Released in 1996, (see criminal prosecution section) it was the company's best selling drug through 2010, when the <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> expired.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Leadership" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>After three generations of Lilly family leadership under company founder, Col. Eli Lilly, his son, Josiah K. Lilly Sr., and two grandsons, Eli Lilly Jr. and Josiah K. Lilly Jr., the company announced a reorganization in 1944 that prepared the way for future expansion and the eventual separation of company management from its ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly112_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly112-134">[134]</a></sup> The large, complex corporation was divided into smaller groups headed by vice presidents and in 1953 Eugene N. Beesley was named the first non-family member to become the company's president.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR6_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR6-69">[69]</a></sup></p><p>Although Lilly family members continued to serve as chairman of the board until 1969, Beesley's appointment began the transition to non-family management.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR6_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR6-69">[69]</a></sup> In 1972 Richard D. Wood became Lilly's president and CEO after the retirement of Burton E. Beck.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> In 1991 Vaughn Bryson became president and Wood became board chairman.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> During Bryson's 20-month tenure as Lilly's president and CEO, the company reported its first quarterly loss as a publicly traded company.<sup id="cite_ref-KokomoTrib62893_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KokomoTrib62893-75">[75]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Randall_L._Tobias" title="Randall L. Tobias">Randall L. Tobias</a>, a vice chairman of <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Corporation" title="AT&amp;T Corporation">AT&amp;T Corporation</a>, was named chairman, president, and CEO in June 1993. Tobias, a Lilly board member since 1986, was recruited from outside the company's executive ranks to replace Lilly's president, Vaughn Bryson, and board chairman, Richard Wood.<sup id="cite_ref-KokomoTrib62893_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KokomoTrib62893-75">[75]</a></sup> Tobias later became the U.S. director of Foreign Assistance and administrator of the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Agency_for_International_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Agency for International Development">U.S. Agency for International Development</a> (USAID), with the rank of ambassador.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Sidney_Taurel" title="Sidney Taurel">Sidney Taurel</a>, former chief operating officer of Lilly, was named CEO in July 1998 to replace Tobias, who retired. Taurel became chairman of the board in January 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR9_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR9-138">[138]</a></sup> Taurel retired as CEO on March 31, 2008, but remained as chairman of the board until December 31, 2008. <a href="/wiki/John_C._Lechleiter" title="John C. Lechleiter">John C. Lechleiter</a> was elected as Lilly's CEO and president, effective April 1, 2008. Lechleiter had served as Lilly's president and chief operating officer since October 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-TaurelRetires_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaurelRetires-139">[139]</a></sup>  In July 2016 Dave Ricks, who also had a long career at Lilly, was appointed CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades">Accolades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Accolades" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<p>In 2006, <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine named Eli Lilly and Company one of the top 100 companies in the United States for which to work. Also in 2006, <i><a href="/wiki/Barron%27s_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Barron's Magazine">Barron's Magazine</a></i> named the company among the top 500 best managed companies in the U.S.
</p><p>In 2012, <i>Working Mothers</i> magazine named Lilly one of the "100 Best Companies for Working Mothers" for the eighteenth consecutive year. <i>Working Mother</i> reported that in 2012 forty-eight percent of Lilly's U.S. employees and thirty-four percent of its U.S. managers and executives were women.<sup id="cite_ref-WorkingMother_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorkingMother-141">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p><p>In 2018, Eli Lilly and Company was recognized as one of the Ethisphere Institute's World's Most Ethical Companies, the second year in a row.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Community_service">Community service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Community service" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>The Lilly family as well as Eli Lilly and Company has a long history of community service. Around 1890 Col. Lilly turned over operation of the family business to his son, Josiah, who ran the company for the next several decades.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR2_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR2-23">[23]</a></sup> Col. Lilly remained active in civic affairs and assisted a number of local organizations, including the Commercial Club of Indianapolis, which later became the Indianapolis Chamber of Commerce,<sup id="cite_ref-b399_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b399-144">[144]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Charity_Organization_Society" title="Charity Organization Society">Charity Organization Society</a>, a forerunner to the Family Services Association of Central Indiana, an organization supported by <a href="/wiki/United_Way_of_America" title="United Way of America">United Way</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR2_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR2-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Josiah's sons, Eli and Joe, were also philanthropists who supported numerous cultural and educational organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-b91012_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b91012-146">[146]</a></sup></p><p>It was Josiah Sr. who continued his father's civic mindedness and began the company tradition of sending aid to disaster victims.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR3_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR3-28">[28]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/1906_San_Francisco_earthquake" title="1906 San Francisco earthquake">1906 San Francisco earthquake</a>, the company sent much needed medicine to support recovery efforts and provided relief after the 1936 <a href="/wiki/Johnstown_Flood" title="Johnstown Flood">Johnstown Flood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR3_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR3-28">[28]</a></sup></p><p>In 1917, Lilly Field Hospital 32, named in Josiah's honor, was equipped in Indianapolis and moved overseas to Contrexville, France, during World War I, where it remained in operation until 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR3_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR3-28">[28]</a></sup> Throughout World War II, Lilly manufactured more than two hundred products for military use, including aviator survival kits and seasickness medications for the D-Day invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-IHSHBR5_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHSHBR5-53">[53]</a></sup> In addition Lilly dried more than two million pints of blood plasma by the war's end.<sup id="cite_ref-Madison,_Eli_Lilly,_p._105_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Madison,_Eli_Lilly,_p._105-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Lilly_Endowment">Lilly Endowment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Lilly Endowment" data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lilly_Endowment" title="Lilly Endowment">Lilly Endowment</a></div>
<p>In 1937, Josiah K. Lilly Sr. and his two sons, Eli and Joe, founded the <a href="/wiki/Lilly_Endowment" title="Lilly Endowment">Lilly Endowment</a>, a private charitable foundation, with gifts of Lilly stock.<sup id="cite_ref-MLilly20506_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MLilly20506-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Eli_Lilly_and_Company_Foundation">Eli Lilly and Company Foundation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Eli Lilly and Company Foundation" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>The Eli Lilly and Company Foundation, which is separate from the Lilly Endowment, operates as a tax-exempt private charitable foundation that the company established in 1968. The Foundation is funded through Lilly's corporate profits.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Controversy" data-section="27" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_pharmaceutical_settlements" title="List of largest pharmaceutical settlements">List of largest pharmaceutical settlements</a></div>
<p>Eli Lilly has been involved in several controversies, including political and medical ethics controversies.  Eli Lilly is now the sole manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Bovine_somatotropin" title="Bovine somatotropin">BGH</a> having purchased the rights to manufacture the drug from <a href="/wiki/Monsanto" title="Monsanto">Monsanto</a>.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Prozac_2">Prozac</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Prozac" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>A landmark study found that <a href="/wiki/Fluoxetine" title="Fluoxetine">fluoxetine</a> (Prozac) was more likely to increase overall suicidal behavior. 14.7% of the patients (n = 44) on fluoxetine had suicidal events, compared to 6.3% in the psychotherapy group and 8.4% from the combined treatment group.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Eli Lilly, the manufacturer, and the lead researcher did not make these findings obvious.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Several internal documents, which were released by the <a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="British Medical Journal">British Medical Journal</a>, indicated a link between use of Prozac and suicidal or violent behavior. The FDA has warned that Prozac and similar antidepressants could cause agitation, panic attacks and aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> These documents revealed that Eli Lilly knew about this as early as 1984, years before the drug was FDA-approved.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="NAFTA_Suit">NAFTA Suit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: NAFTA Suit" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In September 2013, Eli Lilly sued Canada for violating its obligations to foreign investors under the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Agreement</a> by allowing its courts to invalidate patents for two of its drugs.  The company sought damages in the amount of $500 million for lost potential profits.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_prosecution">Criminal prosecution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Criminal prosecution" data-section="30" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Olanzapine#Controversy,_lawsuits_and_settlements" title="Olanzapine">Olanzapine § Controversy, lawsuits and settlements</a>; and <a href="/wiki/James_Gottstein#Eli_Lilly_memos" title="James Gottstein">James Gottstein § Eli Lilly memos</a></div>
<p>In 2009, four sales representatives for Eli Lilly filed separate <a href="/wiki/Qui_tam" title="Qui tam">qui tam</a> lawsuits against the company for illegally marketing olanzapine (branded Zyprexa), an <a href="/wiki/Antipsychotic" title="Antipsychotic">antipsychotic</a> medication, for uses not approved by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a>. Eli Lilly pleaded guilty to actively promoting Zyprexa for <a href="/wiki/Off-label" class="mw-redirect" title="Off-label">off-label</a> uses, particularly for the treatment of dementia in the elderly. The $1.415 billion penalty included an $800 million civil settlement and a $515 million criminal fine. The <a href="/wiki/US_Justice_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="US Justice Department">US Justice Department</a> said the criminal fine of $515 million was the largest ever in a healthcare case and the largest criminal fine for an individual corporation ever imposed in a US criminal prosecution of any kind.<sup id="cite_ref-usdoj-2009_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usdoj-2009-154">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> "That was a blemish for us," <a href="/wiki/John_C._Lechleiter" title="John C. Lechleiter">John C. Lechleiter</a>, CEO of Lilly, told <i>The New York Times</i>. "We don't ever want that to happen again. We put measures in place to assure that not only do we have the right intentions in integrity and compliance, but we have systems in place to support that."<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> In an internal email, Lechleiter had stated "we must seize the opportunity to expand our work with Zyprexa in this same child-adolescent population" for <a href="/wiki/Off-label" class="mw-redirect" title="Off-label">off-label</a> use.<sup id="cite_ref-Berenson_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Berenson-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Insulin_pricing">Insulin pricing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Insulin pricing" data-section="31" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In January 2019, lawmakers from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> sent letters to Eli Lilly and other insulin manufacturers asking for explanations for their rapidly raising insulin prices. The annual cost of insulin for people with type 1 diabetes in the U.S. almost doubled from $2,900 to $5,700 over the period from 2012 to 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eli_Lilly_and_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: References" data-section="32" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">

<li id="cite_note-p58-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-p58_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Price, Nelson (1997). <i>Indiana Legends: Famous Hoosiers from Johnny Appleseed to David Letterman</i>.
